People v. Williams


Docket No. 105453


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


235 Ill. 2d 178; 920 N.E.2d 446; 2009 Ill. LEXIS 1935; 336 Ill. Dec. 237; Copy. L. Rep. (CCH) P29,858; 93 U.S.P.Q.2D (BNA) 1516



November 19, 2009, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Counsel Amended January 25, 2010.


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Williams, 376 Ill. App. 3d 875, 876 N.E.2d 235, 2007 Ill. App. LEXIS 995, 315 Ill. Dec. 235 (Ill. App. Ct. 1st Dist., 2007)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Appellate court judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Illinois Appellate Court affirmed defendant's conviction for not identifying sound recordings with a label containing the actual name and address of the person manufacturing the recording, in violation 720 ILCS 5/16-8 (2004). The state supreme court granted the State's petition for leave to appeal challenging the appellate court's reversal of a separate conviction on preemption grounds. Defendant challenged the affirmance of his conviction.


OVERVIEW: The State charged defendant with violating 720 ILCS 5/16-7 (2004), involving the pirating sound recordings produced by others, and 720 ILCS 5/16-8 (2004), failing to identify sound recordings with a label containing the actual name and address of the person who manufactured the recording. The appellate court reversed the 720 ILCS 5/16-7 (2004) conviction, but then rejected due process and First Amendment challenges to the constitutionality of 720 ILCS 5/16-8 (2004). Regarding 720 ILCS 5/16-7 (2004), the state supreme court found that the presumption of state police power superceding federal law did not apply since the United States Congress expressly preempted state law in that area under 17 U.S.C.S. § 301(a), the federal Copyright Act of 1976. It also affirmed the rejection of defendant's due process and First Amendment challenges to 720 ILCS 5/16-8 (2004). It found that the statute (1) furthered a substantial government interest in protecting the public; (2) restricted First Amendment rights no more broadly than needed to further that interest; and (3) had a reasonable relationship to the interest to be served and used means sufficiently reasonable to accomplish that objective.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court affirmed the appellate court's judgment in all respects.

 

119 of 2788 DOCUMENTS


People v. Graves


Docket No. 106541


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


235 Ill. 2d 244; 919 N.E.2d 906; 2009 Ill. LEXIS 1296; 335 Ill. Dec. 881



September 24, 2009, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Counsel Amended January 25, 2010.
Rehearing denied by People v. Graves, 2009 Ill. LEXIS 1946 (Ill., Nov. 23, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Appellate Court for the First District.
People v. Graves, 2008 Ill. App. LEXIS 2114 (Ill. App. Ct. 1st Dist., Apr. 10, 2008)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Appellate court judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Circuit Court of Cook County, Illinois, convicted defendant of the Class 2 offense of possession of a stolen motor vehicle, 625 ILCS 5/4-103(a)(1), (b) (2006). Defendant was sentenced to a Class X term of nine years based on his prior criminal convictions. The court also imposed several monetary charges. The appellate court affirmed. Defendant petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant asked the supreme court to vacate the two fees and reduce the total assessment by $15 because they violated his federal and state due process rights, U.S. Const. amend. XIV; Ill. Const. art. I, § 2. He argued that the assessment of the fees was unconstitutional because neither charge bore a rational relationship to the offense of possession of a stolen motor vehicle. The supreme court found that the charges imposed did not seek to compensate the state for any costs incurred as the result of prosecuting defendant. The fact that the proceeds of the fines were earmarked for a specific purpose, unrelated to the offense upon which defendant was convicted, was irrelevant to their constitutionality; rather it was because the charges in no way compensated the state for the cost of prosecuting defendant that they were fines, and not fees. The legislature clearly conferred upon county boards the limited power to enact certain fines or penalties, including the mental health court and youth diversion/peer court fines. The $15 in fines imposed as punishment, 55 ILCS 5/5-1101(d-5) and 55 ILCS 5/5-1101(e) (2006), did not violate defendant's due process rights.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

People v. Lovejoy


Docket No. 104443


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


235 Ill. 2d 97; 919 N.E.2d 843; 2009 Ill. LEXIS 1302; 335 Ill. Dec. 818



September 24, 2009, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Counsel Amended January 25, 2010.
Rehearing denied by People v. Lovejoy, 2009 Ill. LEXIS 1945 (Ill., Nov. 23, 2009)
Appeal after remand at People v. Lovejoy, 2013 IL App (2d) 110289, 2013 Ill. App. Unpub. LEXIS 982 (2013)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury found defendant guilty of first degree murder. The Circuit Court of Du Page County (Illinois) sentenced defendant to death. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court erred in denying his request for a continuance to obtain an expert to refute certain testimony of the State's DNA expert which was not disclosed to the defense prior to trial. While the report the expert tendered stated that a test on a tile directly adjacent to defendant's footprint was negative for the presence of blood, the expert testified at trial that the "negative" result was a false negative. The supreme court concluded that defendant was prejudiced by the State's failure to disclose the expert's opinion regarding the false negative. Had defendant been made aware of the expert's conclusion, he could have called an expert to refute that contention or could have chosen to pursue a different line of defense altogether. The supreme court further found that defendant made reasonable efforts to alleviate the prejudice created by seeking a continuance to find an expert, obtaining an expert despite the trial court's denial of his motion, and seeking to reopen his case to include the expert's testimony, but that his attempts were wrongfully thwarted by the trial court. Thus, defendant was entitled to a new trial.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction and sentence were reversed and the case was remanded for a new trial.

On remand at, Decision reached on appeal by People v. Hunt, 2010 Ill. App. LEXIS 866 (Ill. App. Ct. 1st Dist., Aug. 19, 2010)

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Illinois appellate court entered a judgment that affirmed the trial court's ruling that granted defendant's pretrial motions to suppress statements and recordings the State sought to introduce in a case where defendant had been charged with murder. However, it agreed with the trial and appellate court that the recordings were inaudible.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court found the appellate court did not exceed its scope of review on interlocutory appeal. It also reversed the appellate court's judgment affirming suppression of the statements. However, it affirmed the appellate court's judgment upholding suppression of the recordings as substantially inaudible. It then ordered remand of the case to the appellate court for further proceedings regarding suppression of the statements.

In re Marriage of Gulla


Docket No. 106612.


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


234 Ill. 2d 414; 917 N.E.2d 392; 2009 Ill. LEXIS 920; 334 Ill. Dec. 566



June 4, 2009, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Gulla v. Kanaval, 2009 Ill. LEXIS 1308 (Ill., Sept. 28, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Appellate Court for the Second District.
In re Marriage of Gulla, 382 Ill. App. 3d 498, 888 N.E.2d 585, 2008 Ill. App. LEXIS 404, 321 Ill. Dec. 66 (Ill. App. Ct. 2d Dist., 2008)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed as modified; cause remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The trial court found that it had personal jurisdiction over appellant employer, and eventually ordered the employer to pay to appellee mother a penalty for failing to withhold and remit child support from its employee's wages. The trial court calculated the penalty based on 750 ILCS 28/35(a) (2006) and entered judgment of $ 369,000 against the employer. The Appellate Court of Illinois, Second District, affirmed. The employer appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trial court found that the employer failed to withhold any of the father's wages, pursuant to the withholding notice, for over seven months after the notice was served on the employer. Eventually, the trial court imposed a $ 100-per-day penalty for each violation of the Withholding Act, 750 ILCS 28/1 et seq. (2006), pursuant to Illinois law. The mother calculated 3,690 alleged penalties, reflected in the trial court's order, resulting in the judgment of $ 369,000. The appellate court found that this was error. The employer was located in Mississippi, and the penalty for violating the withholding notice should have been calculated based on Mississippi law, Miss. Code Ann. § 93-11-117(1) (2006), which provided for a penalty of not more than $ 500, or $ 1,000 where there was collusion between the employer and employee. The Uniform Interstate Family Support Act established a uniform procedure for enforcing an out-of-state child support order. However, it uniformly directed the employer-violator to the law of its state for the appropriate sanction. Based on this reciprocal statutory scheme, the employer should have been penalized according to the law of its state-Mississippi.


OUTCOME: The case was remanded to the trial court to recalculate the penalty. Otherwise, the judgment was affirmed.

People v. Davis

SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


233 Ill. 2d 244; 909 N.E.2d 766; 2009 Ill. LEXIS 392; 330 Ill. Dec. 744

DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Illinois appellate court entered a judgment that affirmed defendant's convictions on charges of first-degree murder and aggravated battery, and his resulting sentence, after a jury found defendant guilty and defendant appealed. In subsequent proceedings, the trial court found that defendant did not establish a prima facie case of racial discrimination. The state supreme court granted defendant's petition for leave to appeal.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with, inter alia, aggravated battery and first-degree murder for aiding and abetting codefendants in fatally beating a victim with their fists, feet, and a board. During jury selection, the State used a peremptory strike to get excused an African-American prospective juror. It also found him guilty of aggravated burglary. The appellate court affirmed the convictions and sentence. The state supreme court found that the trial court's finding that defendant, an African-American, did not prove a prima facie case of racial discrimination was not clearly erroneous since the Caucasian jurors' responses were justified and instructing the jury on felony murder, where the predicate felony allegedly was inherent in the killing itself was at most harmless error because the jury's general verdict meant that defendant under 720 ILCS 5/9-1(a) (2004) was presumed to be convicted of the most serious form of the offense, intentional murder, rather than felony murder.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court affirmed the appellate court's judgment, which affirmed defendant's convictions and sentence.

April 2, 2009, Opinion Filed

Post-conviction relief dismissed at People v. Titus, 2017 IL App (1st) 150334-U, 2017 Ill. App. Unpub. LEXIS 1361 (June 30, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Smith, 372 Ill. App. 3d 762, 866 N.E.2d 1192, 2007 Ill. App. LEXIS 374, 310 Ill. Dec. 590 (Ill. App. Ct. 1st Dist., Apr. 19, 2007)
People v. Titus, 376 Ill. App. 3d 1140, 952 N.E.2d 730, 2007 Ill. App. LEXIS 4120, 351 Ill. Dec. 938 (Ill. App. Ct. 1st Dist., Oct. 4, 2007)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Appellate court judgments affirmed in part and reversed in part; causes remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Illinois appellate court in two separate cases affirmed the first defendant and second defendant's first-degree murder convictions after the respective jury in each case returned a general guilty verdict despite the charges in each case being based on three different mental states. However, it modified each sentence in favor of the respective defendant. The state supreme court granted the State's petitions for leave to appeal in both cases.


OVERVIEW: In separate cases, the first defendant and second defendant were each charged with intentional murder, knowing murder, and felony murder, and felony offenses underlying the felony-murder charges lodged against them. At their trials, they requested separate verdict forms. The trial courts denied the requests. In both cases, the jury returned general verdicts of guilty of first-degree murder, 720 ILCS 5/9-1(a) (2006), and they were also found guilty of the underlying felony offenses. At sentencing, the trial courts sentenced them on their murder convictions and, additionally, imposed consecutive terms for the underlying felonies. The appellate court affirmed the convictions, but held the trial court had erred by refusing the requests for separate verdict forms and modified the sentences. The state supreme court found that given the unsuccessful requests for separate forms and the jury's guilty finding without specifying which of the mental states set forth in 720 ILCS 5/9-1(a) (2006) the verdicts were based on, knowledge, intent, or felony murder, sentencing had to based on the least serious form of murder, felony murder, as that was the only unquestionable mental state involved.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court affirmed the first defendant's convictions for first-degree murder and armed robbery, and the sentences imposed, but vacated the conviction and sentence imposed for attempted armed robbery. As to the second defendant, it affirmed the first-degree murder conviction and sentence, but vacated the conviction and sentence for armed robbery. It also remanded the cases to the trial courts to correct the relevant mittimuses.

People v. Klepper


Docket No. 105719.


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


234 Ill. 2d 337; 917 N.E.2d 381; 2009 Ill. LEXIS 309; 334 Ill. Dec. 555



March 19, 2009, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Modified Upon Denial of Rehearing September 28, 2009. As Amended November 2, 2009.
Reported at People v. Klepper, 2009 Ill. LEXIS 343 (Ill., Mar. 19, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court of Kendall County.


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a bench trial, the trial court found defendant guilty of felony disorderly conduct under 720 ILCS 5/26-1(a)(4) (2006). The Circuit Court of Kendall County (Illinois) declined to impose a felony sentence, finding a violation of Ill. Const. art. I, § 1, the proportionate penalties clause, and instead imposed a misdemeanor sentence of one year's probation. The State appealed the constitutional ruling directly to the supreme court.


OVERVIEW: After determining that it had jurisdiction to review the State's challenge, the supreme court considered whether 720 ILCS 5/26-1(a)(4) (2006) violated the proportionate penalties clause of the Illinois Constitution, Ill. Const. art. I, § 11. The supreme court held that § 26-1 was not unconstitutional under the "identical elements" test. The supreme court concluded that § 26-1(a)(4) and (a)(12) did not share identical elements, as § 26-1(a)(12) required calling 911, and thus, differing sentencing classifications for a Class 4 felony and a Class A misdemeanor did not offend the proportionate penalties clause of § 11. In response to defendant's other claims, the supreme court found that trial counsel was not ineffective for failing to challenge the sufficiency of the information, as the information contained sufficient particularity to allow defendant to prepare a defense. Defendant's right to confrontation was not violated when the trial court prohibited additional cross examination of defendant's neighbor, as the neighbor's bias was explored during cross-examination, and defendant could not show prejudice, as he would have been convicted even if the offer of proof had been made.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed.

Burnette v. Terrell


Docket No. 106678.


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


232 Ill. 2d 522; 905 N.E.2d 816; 2009 Ill. LEXIS 307; 328 Ill. Dec. 927



March 19, 2009, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at Burnette v. Terrell, 2009 Ill. LEXIS 370 (Ill., Mar. 19, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted leave to petitioner, a county public defender, who filed an original action, pursuant to Ill. Sup. Ct. R. 381, seeking either a writ of mandamus or a writ of prohibition against respondent, a circuit court judge. The controversy involved the judge's repeated removal of an individual assistant public defender from representing clients in his courtroom.


OVERVIEW: Respondent repeatedly recused himself from cases in which the assistant public defender represented the defendants in his courtroom and also directed her repeatedly to step down from serving as the assigned public defender in his courtroom for defendants she had already been representing. The Court held that the issue was not mooted since the manner in which respondent assigned cases to assistant public defenders in individual cases was still at issue. The court held that a judge had the authority to remove a public defender from a case and assign another under certain circumstances, but that such circumstances were not shown in the case at bar. The court held in the absence of respondent finding the assistant public defender at issue in contempt or having other cause, his actions were not within his inherent power to manage his courtroom and calendar and were directed at an individual attorney, which infringed upon petitioner's statutory authority. The court further noted that respondent failed to make a record sufficient to justify his actions. The court discerned that neither mandamus nor prohibition were appropriate remedies and, instead, set forth a supervisory order.


OUTCOME: The court ordered respondent to follow specific directives it set forth when he determined that an indigent defendant was entitled to appointed counsel in his courtroom.

People v. Cardamone


Docket No. 106200.


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


232 Ill. 2d 504; 905 N.E.2d 806; 2009 Ill. LEXIS 304; 328 Ill. Dec. 917



March 19, 2009, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at People v. Cardamone, 2009 Ill. LEXIS 362 (Ill., Mar. 19, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Cardamone, 379 Ill. App. 3d 656, 883 N.E.2d 628, 2008 Ill. App. LEXIS 105, 318 Ill. Dec. 397 (Ill. App. Ct. 2d Dist., 2008)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction of harassment of a witness in violation of 720 ILCS 5/32-4a (2004). The Appellate Court (Illinois) affirmed his conviction. He appealed that decision arguing the State failed to establish that the target of his harassment experienced a level of distress akin to a threat of physical damage to person or property or that he intended to communicate with his target.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and the witness both attended a hearing in another criminal proceeding where defendant was accused of wrongdoing. The witness was both a potential witness and the mother of an alleged victim. After the hearing, defendant and the witness both took the same road to get from the courthouse to their respective homes. Defendant phoned 911 to report the witness as a drunk driver. He gave the 911 operator the witness's license plate number and stated that he saw a half-covered bottle in the car. The witness was subsequently stopped by a police officer. The officer did not notice any erratic driving or any signs of intoxication or drinking. The officer saw no beverage container. The Illinois Supreme Court held the witness's testimony as to having suffered stomach dropping, nerve racking, wondering what she had done wrong, knowing that she had not done anything wrong, and anger were included within the scope of 720 ILCS 5/32-4a. Having found that the statute incorporated a more expansive definition of "emotional distress" than defendant contended, the State met its burden of proof. As defendant hoped that the witness would be stopped, the intent-to-communicate element was met.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the lower appellate court was affirmed.

 

130 of 2788 DOCUMENTS


Adames v. Sheahan


Docket Nos. 105789, 105851 cons.


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


233 Ill. 2d 276; 909 N.E.2d 742; 2009 Ill. LEXIS 310; 330 Ill. Dec. 720



March 19, 2009, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at Adames v. Sheahan, 2009 Ill. LEXIS 356 (Ill., Mar. 19, 2009)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by, Motion granted by Adames v. Beretta U.S.A. Corp., 175 L. Ed. 2d 634, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 9085 (U.S., 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: Adames v. Sheahan, 378 Ill. App. 3d 502, 880 N.E.2d 559, 2007 Ill. App. LEXIS 1263, 316 Ill. Dec. 823 (Ill. App. Ct. 1st Dist., 2007)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Appellate court judgment affirmed in part and reversed in part; circuit court judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee estate filed an action against appellants, a sheriff and a manufacturer. Following discovery, the trial court granted summary judgment in favor of appellants. The Appellate Court (Illinois) reversed the grant of summary judgment to the sheriff on the issue of respondeat superior and to the manufacturer with regard to the duty to warn claim, but affirmed the remainder of the trial court's order. The sheriff and the manufacturer appealed.


OVERVIEW: The estate's decedent, a child, was killed when a friend who was playing with the service weapon of his father shot him. The estate sued the sheriff based on a respondeat superior theory and sued the manufacturer based on a strict products liability theory. The supreme court found error in the appellate court's determination that the father was acting within the scope of his employment when he stored his weapon and that the sheriff was liable for the father's tortious acts. The father, a correctional officer, was not required to own a gun and did not need to carry it to work. The father's negligent storage of the gun was not the kind of conduct that he was employed to perform, nor was it incidental to his employment. With regard to the manufacturer, summary judgment was proper as to the defective design claim because, inter alia, the gun performed as expected and was not unreasonably dangerous for its intended use as a law enforcement service weapon. The failure to warn claim against the manufacturer was barred by the Protection of Lawful Commerce in Arms Act, 15 U.S.C.S. §§ 7901-7903, as the discharge of the gun was caused by a volitional act that constituted a criminal offense.


OUTCOME: The appellate court's reversal of the grant of summary judgment on the issues of respondeat superior and duty to warn was reversed. The appellate court's judgment was otherwise affirmed. The trial court's judgment was affirmed.

 

131 of 2788 DOCUMENTS


Pooh-Bah Enters. v. County of Cook


Docket Nos. 105971, 105984 cons.


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


232 Ill. 2d 463; 905 N.E.2d 781; 2009 Ill. LEXIS 308; 328 Ill. Dec. 892



March 19, 2009, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at Pooh-Bah Enters. v. County of Cook, 2009 Ill. LEXIS 360 (Ill., Mar. 19, 2009)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Poo-Bah Enters. v. Cook County, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 6221 (U.S., Oct. 5, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: Pooh-Bah Enters. v. County of Cook, 378 Ill. App. 3d 268, 881 N.E.2d 552, 2007 Ill. App. LEXIS 1354, 317 Ill. Dec. 384 (Ill. App. Ct. 1st Dist., 2007)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Appellate court judgment reversed; circuit court judgment affirmed in part; cause remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a county and a city, challenged an Illinois appellate court's reversal of a ruling that upheld a small venue exemption, on grounds that amusement tax ordinances (Chicago, Ill., Mun. Code § 4-156-020(A) (2008) and Cook County, Ill., Amusement Tax Ordinance § 3 (1999)) were not overbroad nor vague, but granted appellants' 735 ILCS 5/2-615 (2006) motion to dismiss appellee business owner's complaint for declaratory and injunctive relief.


OVERVIEW: The ordinances drew a clear distinction between two entirely different types of entertainment. The exemptions fell within the authority of the government to make content-based distinctions when it subsidized speech, and the owner could not plead any set of facts that demonstrated otherwise. The subsidy was in the public interest--encouraging fine arts performances in small venues--and was defined in such a way to achieve those ends. It was logical to exclude categories of protected speech that did not advance its goals, e.g., movies, television, promotional shows, performances at adult entertainment cabarets, and performances in venues that seated more than 750 persons. The subsidy of all productions at small fine arts venues had no minimum standards, and no considerations of viewpoint were taken into account. The subsidy was not aimed at suppressing dangerous ideas. The activities in the owner's business were neither fine arts nor cultural; the activities presented an entirely different type of entertainment. Thus, denying adult entertainment cabarets an exemption reserved for small fine arts venues was reasonable and did not offend the state or federal constitutions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, reversed the appellate court's judgment and remanded the cause to the appellate court for consideration of the owner's remaining claims.

People v. M.W. (In re M.W.)


Docket No. 104519


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


232 Ill. 2d 408; 905 N.E.2d 757; 2009 Ill. LEXIS 171; 328 Ill. Dec. 868



January 23, 2009, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by People v. M.W. (In re M.W.), 2009 Ill. LEXIS 2351 (Ill., Mar. 23, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. M.W. (In re M.W.), 225 Ill. 2d 635, 875 N.E.2d 1112, 2007 Ill. LEXIS 1181, 314 Ill. Dec. 825 (2007)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Appellate court judgment reversed; circuit court judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State of Illinois filed a petition for adjudication of wardship pursuant to § 2-13 of the Illinois Juvenile Court Act of 1987, 705 ILCS 405/2-13 (1998). A circuit court adjudicated appellee minor delinquent. The Illinois Appellate Court vacated the order. The State filed a petition for leave to appeal pursuant to Ill. Sup. Ct. R. 315.


OVERVIEW: The minor argued that the adjudication of delinquency was void for lack of subject matter jurisdiction because her father was not served with written notice that the petition by the State of Illinois was amended to add an additional charge, in violation of § 5-530 of the Illinois Juvenile Court Act of 1987, 705 ILCS 405/5-530 (2004). On appeal, the court found that the intermediate appellate court incorrectly concluded that compliance with the notice requirements of the Act was an unwaivable condition precedent to the circuit court's exercise of subject matter jurisdiction over the justiciable matter before it. When the minor's parents appeared at the detention hearing and were given a copies of the petition, personal jurisdiction over them was established under 705 ILCS 405/5-525(4) (2004). Further, the State's failure to give notice of an amendment to the father under 705 ILCS 405/5-530 did not cause the circuit court to lose jurisdiction. Finally, the evidence that the minor participated in the aggravated battery of the victim under 720 ILCS 5/5-2 (2004) was not so closely balanced that she was entitled to a new hearing on that charge on the basis of plain error.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was reversed, and the judgment of the circuit court was affirmed.

Cook County Republican Party v. Ill. State Bd. of Elections


Docket No. 106139


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


232 Ill. 2d 231; 902 N.E.2d 652; 2009 Ill. LEXIS 174; 327 Ill. Dec. 531



January 23, 2009, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at Cook County Republican Party v. State Bd. of Elections, 2009 Ill. App. LEXIS 1256 (Ill. App. Ct. 1st Dist., Dec. 18, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Appellate Court for the First District.
Cook County Republican Party v. State Bd. of Elections, 378 Ill. App. 3d 752, 882 N.E.2d 93, 2007 Ill. App. LEXIS 1295, 317 Ill. Dec. 519 (Ill. App. Ct. 1st Dist., 2007)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county political party filed complaints with appellee state board of elections alleging violations of the Election Code (10 ILCS 5/1-1 et seq. (2004)), by appellee party ward organizations, chairmen, and committeemen. The board dismissed the complaints on tie votes. An appellate court (Illinois) found that the dismissals were subject only to limited judicial review of whether the board acted contrary to law. Appellants sought review.


OVERVIEW: The board did not determine that the complaints were filed on justifiable grounds when it voted four-to-four on that question; thus, the Board complied with the clear directive of 10 ILCS 5/9-21 (2004) by dismissing the complaints. The tie-vote dismissals were subject to judicial review under the plain language of 10 ILCS 5/9-22 (2004) and the provisions of the Administrative Review Law, 735 ILCS 5/3-110 (2004). The scope of review extended to all questions of law and fact presented by the entire record before the court. Dismissal of the complaints could be reviewed by considering whether the members voting to dismiss clearly erred in determining that they were not filed on justifiable grounds. The reasons for dismissal adopted from the general counsel's recommendation was reviewable for clear error. The dismissals could not be overturned unless the court was left with a definite and firm conviction that a mistake was committed. Given the construction of the Code providing for judicial review of deadlock dismissals, it was unnecessary to consider the arguments challenging the Code's constitutionality or whether those arguments were procedurally defaulted.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's judgment affirming the dismissals of the complaints and remanded the matter to the appellate court to conduct the judicial review of the dismissals consistent with the court's opinion.

 

134 of 2788 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Madigan v. Kinzer


Docket No. 105805


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


232 Ill. 2d 179; 902 N.E.2d 667; 2009 Ill. LEXIS 172; 327 Ill. Dec. 546



January 23, 2009, Opinion Filed


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Mandamus awarded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the Illinois Attorney General (AG) on behalf of the People of the State of Illinois, filed an original mandamus action seeking to vacate a sentence of court supervision imposed on respondent driver by respondent judge for the driver's DUI conviction. The AG contended that court supervision was prohibited by 730 ILCS 5/5-6-1(d)(3) (2006) because the driver previously pled guilty to reckless driving as a result of a plea agreement.


OVERVIEW: The judge contended that § 5-6-1(d)(3) prohibited the imposition of court supervision only if a defendant had previously pled guilty to reckless driving as a result of a plea agreement that reduced a charge of DUI. The supreme court disagreed. Under the plain language of § 5-6-1(d)(3), a previous guilty plea to reckless driving under a plea agreement was the only requirement for barring a sentence of court supervision. There was no requirement for the plea agreement to reduce a charge of driving under the influence of alcohol to reckless driving. The docket sheet from the driver's prior conviction showed that he pled guilty to reckless driving prior to trial and that by agreement the State moved to nol-pros the DUI charge. The driver received consideration in exchange for his guilty plea to reckless driving based on the State's agreement to dismiss the DUI charge. The record, therefore, clearly established that the driver's guilty plea was the result of a plea agreement. Accordingly, § 5-6-1(d)(3) precluded a sentence of court supervision for the driver's subsequent DUI conviction. The judge had no discretion to depart from the mandatory provisions of § 5-6-1(d)(3).


OUTCOME: The supreme court granted mandamus relief, directing the judge to vacate the sentence of court supervision and to impose a sentence in accord with the Unified Code of Corrections.

 

135 of 2788 DOCUMENTS


People v. Artis


Docket No. 105751


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


232 Ill. 2d 156; 902 N.E.2d 677; 2009 Ill. LEXIS 180; 327 Ill. Dec. 556



January 23, 2009, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Appellate Court for the Third District.
People v. Artis, 377 Ill. App. 3d 216, 879 N.E.2d 427, 2007 Ill. App. LEXIS 1192, 316 Ill. Dec. 360 (Ill. App. Ct. 3d Dist., 2007)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Appellate court judgment affirmed in part and reversed in part; cause remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pled guilty to sexual assault during a residential burglary and home invasion, residential burglary, home invasion, and unlawful restraint, violations of 720 ILCS 5/12-14(a)(4), 12-11(a)(2), 19-3(a) and 10-3(a) (2002). An appellate court (Illinois) vacated the sexual assault and home invasion convictions and the extended-term sentence for unlawful restraint, and reduced the sentence to the maximum nonextended term. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The State argued for the abolishment of the one-act, one-crime doctrine because the rationale for the doctrine no longer existed because parole was abolished. In light of the court's treatment of one-act, one-crime violations as adversely affecting the integrity of the judicial process under the second prong of the plain-error rule, the court declined to abandon the doctrine. The court held that where there were multiple convictions for aggravated criminal sexual assault based upon the same physical act, none of the offenses were more serious than any other--none of the offenses involved a more culpable or less culpable mental state. The State had the opportunity to argue to the appellate court that there was no one-act, one-crime violation and that defendant was properly convicted of both sexual assault offenses. Instead, the State conceded that one of the convictions had to be vacated on one-act, one-crime principles. The State did not raise the issue in its petition for leave to appeal. There was no basis to ignore the State's forfeiture of the issue.


OUTCOME: The court remanded the cause to the trial court for a determination as to which sexual assault conviction would be retained and for resentencing in accordance with 730 ILCS 5/5-8-4 (2002). The court affirmed the remainder of the appellate court's judgment.

Morr-Fitz, Inc. v. Blagojevich


Docket No. 104692


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


231 Ill. 2d 474; 901 N.E.2d 373; 2008 Ill. LEXIS 1834; 327 Ill. Dec. 45



December 18, 2008, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at Morr-Fitz, Inc. v. Quinn, 2012 Ill. App. LEXIS 783, 2012 IL App (4th) 110398 (2012)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Appellate Court for the Fourth District.
Morr-Fitz, Inc. v. Blagojevich, 2008 Ill. LEXIS 1829 (Ill., Dec. 18, 2008)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgments reversed; cause remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, pharmacists and pharmacy owners and operators, challenged a decision of an appellate court (Illinois) that affirmed the dismissal of their declaratory judgment suit against appellees, state officials and the Board of Pharmacy, to invalidate a rule that violated the Health Care Right of Conscience Act (745 ILCS 70/1 et seq. (2004)), the Religious Freedom Restoration Act (775 ILCS 35/1 et seq. (2004)), and U.S. Const. amend, I.


OVERVIEW: Appellants claimed that Ill. Admin. Code tit. 68, § 1330.91(j), requiring dispensing of Plan B contraceptives, violated their moral and religious beliefs that life began at conception and that Plan B acted as an abortifacient. The court found that the claims were ripe under Abbott Laboratories. The claims were legal in nature. Sufficient hardship existed so as to make judicial review appropriate. The rule's revised form was more coercive in that it required the stocking and dispensing of Plan B contraception. A failure to make efforts to stock the contraceptive subjected violators to penalties, including license revocation. Application of the rule to appellants was not remote; they had already suffered financial loss, and the rule chilled their First Amendment rights by forcing them to comply or compromise their rights to act according to their consciences and religious tenets. Appellants did not have to exhaust administrative remedies because the Pharmacy Act of 1987, 225 ILCS 85/11 et seq. (2004), did not provide for review. Because the case involved a question of law that did not involve agency expertise, appellants did not have to seek a variance before suing in circuit court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgments of the appellate and circuit courts and remanded the cause to the circuit court for further proceedings consistent with the court's opinion.

Ready v. United/Goedecke Servs.


Docket No. 103474


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


2008 Ill. LEXIS 1439



November 25, 2008, Opinion Filed


NOTICE:

THIS DECISION IS NOT FINAL UNTIL EXPIRATION OF THE 21 DAY PETITION FOR REHEARING PERIOD.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Modified and rehearing denied by Ready v. United/Goedecke Servs., 2008 Ill. LEXIS 1837 (Ill., Nov. 25, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: Ready v. United/Goedecke Servs., 367 Ill. App. 3d 272, 854 N.E.2d 758, 2006 Ill. App. LEXIS 744, 305 Ill. Dec. 166 (Ill. App. Ct. 1st Dist., 2006)


DISPOSITION:  [*1] Appellate court judgment affirmed in part and reversed in part; circuit court judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff widow brought a wrongful death action against defendants, a subcontractor and two companies that ultimately settled with plaintiff. After judgment was entered against the subcontractor, the Illinois Appellate Court remanded for a new trial on liability only, directing that fault for the accident be reapportioned under Ill. Rev. Stat. ch. 110, para. 2-1117 (1987). Plaintiff appealed.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court held that under § 2-1117, a nonsettling defendant's fault should be assessed relative to the fault of all defendants, including settling ones. Holding that § 2-1117 was ambiguous, the court concluded that § 2-1117, as enacted in 1986, was never intended to include settling tortfeasors in the apportionment of fault. In 1995, the appellate court had held that settling defendants were not to be included in the apportionment of fault; the legislature's failure to address this holding in 2003, when it amended the statute in other respects, indicated its acceptance of this judicial interpretation. Furthermore, under amendments in 1995, which were ultimately held unconstitutional, settling tortfeasors were to be included on the verdict form. This constituted a recognition that they were not originally included in the apportionment of fault. Next, the court could not review the subcontractor's argument that in upholding the amount of the damages award, the appellate court erred in applying procedural default. Review would require examination of the briefs filed in the appellate court, which had not been made part of the record pursuant to Ill. Sup. Ct. R. 318(c).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court upholding the amount of the damages award. It reversed the remainder of the appellate court's judgment and affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

Ready v. United/Goedecke Servs.


Docket No. 103474


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


232 Ill. 2d 369; 905 N.E.2d 725; 2008 Ill. LEXIS 1837; 328 Ill. Dec. 836



November 25, 2008, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Modified on Denial of Rehearing March 23, 2009.
Appeal after remand at, Remanded by Ready v. United/Goedecke Servs., 2009 Ill. App. LEXIS 589 (Ill. App. Ct. 1st Dist., June 30, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: Ready v. United/Goedecke Servs., 2008 Ill. LEXIS 1439 (Ill., Nov. 25, 2008)
Ready v. United/Goedecke Servs., 367 Ill. App. 3d 272, 854 N.E.2d 758, 2006 Ill. App. LEXIS 744, 305 Ill. Dec. 166 (Ill. App. Ct. 1st Dist., 2006)



DISPOSITION: Appellate court judgment affirmed in part and reversed in part; cause remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant administrator sued, among others, appellee subcontractor, seeking damages for the alleged wrongful death of the administrator's decedent. After the other defendants settled, a jury awarded damages to the administrator. The Appellate Court of Illinois, First District, Third Division, affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded, directing that fault for the accident be reapportioned. The administrator appealed.


OVERVIEW: The subcontractor argued that the trial court erred by failing to include the settling tortfeasors on the verdict form so that the jury could have determined their share of fault. The issue on appeal was whether the settling tortfeasors were defendants sued by the administrator within the meaning of former 735 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/2-1117. The appellate court found that the statute was ambiguous with regard to whether it included within its scope settling tortfeasors. However, an earlier appellate decision had held that, under 735 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/2-1117, settling defendants were not to be included in the apportionment of fault, and a later amendment to the statute did not deal with this prior holding. This indicated that the legislature accepted this judicial interpretation. Former 735 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/2-1117, as enacted in 1986, did not apply to good-faith settling tortfeasors who had been dismissed from the suit. The subcontractor's argument that it was deprived of a sole proximate cause defense was raised in the intermediate appellate court, but was not addressed by that court in light of its remand order.


OUTCOME: The portion of the intermediate appellate court's judgment reversing the trial court as to liability was reversed. The judgment of the intermediate appellate court upholding the amount of the damages award was affirmed. The case was remanded to the intermediate appellate court for a decision on the subcontractor's claim that the jury should have been instructed on sole proximate cause.

Wackrow v. Niemi


Docket No. 105459


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


231 Ill. 2d 418; 899 N.E.2d 273; 2008 Ill. LEXIS 1434; 326 Ill. Dec. 56



November 20, 2008, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Appellate Court for the First District.
Wackrow v. Niemi, 2007 Ill. App. LEXIS 4001 (Ill. App. Ct. 1st Dist., Aug. 31, 2007)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Appellate court judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant beneficiary filed a complaint against appellee attorney in the circuit court for legal malpractice. The trial court dismissed the complaint as time-barred under § 13-214.3(d) of the Illinois Code of Civil Procedure, 735 ILCS 5/13-214.3(d) (1994). The Appellate Court of Illinois affirmed the trial court's judgment. The beneficiary appealed pursuant to Ill. Sup. Ct. R. 315(a).


OVERVIEW: The attorney, who prepared a living trust for the beneficiary's brother, prepared an amendment to the living trust which conveyed the brother's residence to the beneficiary. When the brother died, the probate court denied the beneficiary's claim against her brother's estate. The beneficiary alleged that the attorney failed to exercise reasonable care in creating the trust amendment, because a sufficient title search would have revealed that the actual owner of the property was not the brother individually, but another trust. The state supreme court found that the beneficiary alleged legal malpractice in the drafting of the amendment to her brother's trust. Because the brother could have revoked that amendment or changed the beneficiary prior to his death, the injury did not occur until his death. Consequently, 735 ILCS 5/13-214.3(d) applied to the beneficiary's claim. Further, because letters of office were issued and the brother's will was admitted to probate, the beneficiary was required to file her complaint within the time for filing claims against the estate or the time for filing a petition contesting the validity of the will. However, the beneficiary failed to do so.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was affirmed.

People v. Rolandis G. (In re Rolandis G.)


Docket No. 99581


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


232 Ill. 2d 13; 902 N.E.2d 600; 2008 Ill. LEXIS 1440; 327 Ill. Dec. 479



November 20, 2008, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by People v. Rolandis G. (In re Rolandis G.), 2009 Ill. LEXIS 100 (Ill., Jan. 26, 2009)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Rolandis G. v. Ill., 2009 U.S. LEXIS 4027 (U.S., June 1, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Appeal Court for the Second District.
People v. Rolandis G. (In re Rolandis G.), 352 Ill. App. 3d 776, 817 N.E.2d 183, 2004 Ill. App. LEXIS 1260, 288 Ill. Dec. 58 (Ill. App. Ct., 2004)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Appellate court judgment reversed; cause remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent juvenile was adjudicated delinquent after being convicted of aggravated criminal sexual assault of a six-year-old child, a violation of 720 ILCS 5/12-14(b)(i) (2002). The appellate court (Illinois) reversed the adjudication and remanded for a new trial, ruling that hearsay testimony of two witnesses had been improperly admitted under Crawford v. Washington and that 725 ILCS 5/115-10 (2002) was unconstitutional. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trial court admitted into evidence hearsay statements the child made to his mother and a videotaped statement the child made to a child advocate. The child identified respondent in court, but refused to testify regarding the offense charged. The trial court ruled under § 115-10(b)(1) that the time, content, and circumstances surrounding the child's statements provided sufficient indicia of reliability, and that the child had testified within the meaning of § 115-10(b)(2)(A). The appellate court reversed, holding: (1) the statements were inadmissible under Crawford because they were for investigative purposes and testimonial in nature, the child did not testify at trial and respondent had no opportunity to cross-examine; and (2) § 115-10 was unconstitutional to the extent that it permitted introduction of testimonial statements in violation of the Confrontation Clause. The supreme court agreed that the videotaped statement was admitted in violation of respondent's confrontation rights but that the error was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt because properly admitted evidence overwhelmingly supported the conviction. The State withdrew its challenge concerning § 115-10.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court judgment, which reversed respondent's adjudication, and remanded the case to the circuit court with instructions that respondent's adjudication be reinstated.

Bd. of Educ. v. Bd. of Educ.


Docket No. 105018


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


231 Ill. 2d 184; 897 N.E.2d 756; 2008 Ill. LEXIS 1423; 325 Ill. Dec. 217



October 17, 2008, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Appellate Court for the Third District
Bd. of Educ. v. Bd. of Educ., 373 Ill. App. 3d 563, 868 N.E.2d 337, 2007 Ill. App. LEXIS 378, 311 Ill. Dec. 337 (Ill. App. Ct. 3d Dist., 2007)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the Illinois State Board of Education, filed a petition for leave to appeal after an Illinois appellate court ruled that 105 ILCS 5/7-2b (1998) was preempted by the Equal Educational Opportunities Act of 1974 (EEOA), 20 U.S.C.S. §1701 et seq., and remanded a school district annexation matter to the Board for consideration of the EEOA claim made by appellee, the Board of Education, Joliet Township High School District No. 204.


OVERVIEW: District 204 raised its EEOA claim as an objection to a petition under § 7-2b to detach a parcel of land from District 204 and annex it to a second high school district. District 204 argued that the detachement/annexation (D/A) increased segregation in violation of the EEOA. The EEOA prohibited educational agencies in acting in discriminatory ways, but there was no language in the EEOA that required any state or "educational agency" to consider the segregative effects of their actions prior to taking them. Further, due to limiting language in § 7-2b, neither the hearing officer nor the Board could consider the EEO claim in conjunction with the § 7-2b petition. As a result, the circuit court could not consider the EEOA claim as part of its administrative review of the D/A decision. The circuit court could, however, consider the EEOA claim under its Ill. Const. art. VI, § 9 grant of original jurisdiction over justiciable matters. Given the nature of the claim, District 204 could seek an injunction pending resolution of the claim in the circuit court. Thus, § 7-b2 did not conflict with the purposes and objectives of the EEOA, and the EEOA did not preempt § 7-2b.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed that portion of the appellate court's decision that held that § 7-2b was preempted by the EEOA and remanded the matter to the circuit court.

Mikolajczyk v. Ford Motor Co.


Docket No. 104983


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


231 Ill. 2d 516; 901 N.E.2d 329; 2008 Ill. LEXIS 1424; 327 Ill. Dec. 1



October 17, 2008, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Dissenting Opinion at Mikolajczyk v. Ford Motor Co., 231 Ill. 2d 516, 901 N.E.2d 329, 2008 Ill. LEXIS 1826, 327 Ill. Dec. 1 (Ill., Dec. 18, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Appellate Court for the First District
Mikolajczyk v. Ford Motor Co., 374 Ill. App. 3d 646, 870 N.E.2d 885, 2007 Ill. App. LEXIS 640, 312 Ill. Dec. 441 (Ill. App. Ct. 1st Dist., 2007)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Appellate court judgment reversed; circuit court judgment reversed; cause remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, an automaker and the designer of the seats in an auto, were granted leave to appeal an Illinois appellate court decision that affirmed a judgment against defendants in a product liability action filed by plaintiff, the special administrator of a decedent's estate but reversed an award of damages for loss of society and remanded for a remittitur determination. Plaintiff was allowed to petition for cross-relief on the damages issue.


OVERVIEW: The case was a defective design case dealing with the driver's seat in a car. The supreme court rejected defendants' assertion, the risk-utility test was the sole measure of the dangerousness of an allegedly defectively designed product in all strict liability cases or in a subset of those cases. Both the consumer-expectation test and the risk-utility test applied in design defect cases. However, the supreme court did adopt an integrated risk-utility test that included consumer expectation as an element of the broader risk-utility test. The consumer-expectation test and the risk-utility test were methods of proof, not theories of liability. In the instant case, there was evidence to support defendants' position that under the risk-utility test, the seat was not unreasonably dangerous. Because there was no pattern instruction on the risk-utility test, defendants tendered a non-pattern instruction that the trial court declined to give. Defendants were entitled to a risk-utility instruction, and therefore, were entitled to a new trial on liability. As a new trial was necessary, the supreme court did not reach the question of whether the damage award was excessive.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the appellate court's judgment, reversed the circuit court's judgment, and remanded the cause for a new trial.

 

143 of 2788 DOCUMENTS


People v. Galan


Docket No. 103845


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


229 Ill. 2d 484; 893 N.E.2d 597; 2008 Ill. LEXIS 639; 323 Ill. Dec. 325



July 24, 2008, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Counsel Amended September 19, 2008.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court of Cook County. No. AC1-04-2612. Honorable Fred G. Suria Jr., Judge, Presiding.
People v. Galan, 367 Ill. App. 3d 876, 856 N.E.2d 511, 2006 Ill. App. LEXIS 868, 305 Ill. Dec. 706 (Ill. App. Ct. 1st Dist., 2006)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was indicted for possession with intent to deliver 900 or more grams of cocaine and more than 5000 grams of cannabis. Defendant argued that his arrest by Chicago police was illegal because it took place in Indiana. The trial court, vacating a prior order, granted his motion to suppress. The State filed an interlocutory appeal. The Illinois Appellate Court affirmed the trial court's decision. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The court held that the arrest did not violate the Fourth Amendment. The Chicago police were in fresh pursuit of a person they suspected to be a fleeing felon; thus, they had the common-law authority to effect an arrest outside the limits of Illinois. Aside from the fact that the Chicago police failed to comply with the magistrate provision of Ind. Code Ann. § 35-33-3-2 (1998), defendant's arrest was valid. Although defendant was not brought before an Indiana magistrate, he was promptly taken before an Illinois judge; thus, his due process rights were not violated. Furthermore, the violation of § 35-33-3-2 itself did not mandate exclusion. The statutory language did not impose such a requirement, and the statute did not involve a fundamental right of defendant. The arrest did not involve any police misconduct, willful disregard for the laws of Indiana or its territorial borders, or false representations of law; the officers were not aware they were in Indiana. Furthermore, comity did not require exclusion. The Indiana Supreme Court had held that a trial court's jurisdiction did not depend upon the legality of a defendant's arrest or return to the wanting state.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court affirming the decision of the trial court. It reinstated the trial court's original determination that defendant's motion to quash and suppress be denied and remanded the cause to the trial court for trial.

 

144 of 2788 DOCUMENTS


People v. Brown


Docket No. 104375


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


229 Ill. 2d 374; 892 N.E.2d 1034; 2008 Ill. LEXIS 627; 323 Ill. Dec. 42



June 19, 2008, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Appellate Court for the Second District.
People v. Brown, 371 Ill. App. 3d 450, 863 N.E.2d 283, 2007 Ill. App. LEXIS 89, 309 Ill. Dec. 53 (Ill. App. Ct. 2d Dist., 2007)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Appellate court judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was found guilty of home invasion, a violation of 720 ILCS 5/12-11(a)(1) (2002), a Class X felony. He was sentenced to natural life imprisonment after he was adjudged an habitual criminal pursuant to the Habitual Criminal Act, 720 ILCS 5/33B-1 et seq. (2002). The Appellate Court (Illinois) affirmed the sentence. Defendant appealed, seeking a remand on grounds that the trial court lacked authority to sentence him as an habitual criminal.


OVERVIEW: The court adhered to its holding in Robinson that § 33B-2(b) created a permissive rebuttable presumption of eligibility and held that the Act required the State to prove defendant's eligibility for habitual criminal sentencing by showing, by a preponderance of the evidence, that he had three convictions for qualifying offenses that met the time and sequence requirements of § 33B-1(d). Certified records of the convictions, as prima facie evidence, created an inference or rebuttable presumption that he was eligible for habitual criminal sentencing. Defendant had no actual burden to produce any evidence at his sentencing hearing; but, once the State produced prima facie evidence, the sentencing court was permitted to find that the State had met its burden of persuasion. Pursuant to § 33B-2, defendant forfeited the right to challenge his habitual criminal sentence on appeal because he offered no evidence to rebut the presumption of eligibility and admitted that he was eligible. The sentencing court was authorized to find that the State had met its burden of persuasion and the habitual criminal sentence imposed by the court is not void.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's judgment upholding defendant's life sentence.

 

145 of 2788 DOCUMENTS


O'Casek v. Children's Home & Aid Soc'y


Docket No. 105050


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


229 Ill. 2d 421; 892 N.E.2d 994; 2008 Ill. LEXIS 628; 323 Ill. Dec. 2



June 19, 2008, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Appellate Court for the Fourth District.
O'Casek v. Childrens Home & Aid Soc'y, 374 Ill. App. 3d 507, 874 N.E.2d 150, 2007 Ill. App. LEXIS 687, 314 Ill. Dec. 240 (Ill. App. Ct. 4th Dist., 2007)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a health care system and two doctors, appealed a judgment of the Illinois Court of Appeals for the Fourth District, which reversed the dismissal of the medical malpractice action filed by plaintiff, the special administrator of a decedent's estate. The complaint, refiled after a voluntary dismissal, had been dismissed for failure to serve a 735 ILCS 5/2-622 (2002) certificate of merit with the complaint.


OVERVIEW: The case turned on the question of whether 1997 Ill. Laws 579 (PA 90-579) had reenacted the 1995 version of § 2-622, which had been struck down by the Best decision along with the rest of the 1995 Civil Justice Reform Amendments (CJRA). The 1995 version of § 2-622 precluded a plaintiff from obtaining a 90-day extension for filing a certificate of merit if a case had previously been voluntarily dismissed. The pre-1995 version of § 2-622 did not include that restriction The supreme court held that PA 90-579 did not reenact the 1995 version of § 2-622. To the extent that Cargill v. Czelatdko, 353 Ill. App. 3d 654 (2004), Crull v. Sriratana, 376 Ill. App. 3d 803 (2007), and other cases held otherwise, they were overruled. Under 25 ILCS 10/10(f) (2004), 5 ILCS 70/5 (2004), new language amending a statute was to be underscored or italicized. The only such words in P.A. 90-579 were words adding naprapaths to the covered health professionals list. The legislative history of P.A. 90-579 also supported a finding that it was not intended to reenact the 1995 version of § 2-622. Thus, the pre-1995 version of § 2-622, as amended by P.A. 90-579 to include narapraths, applied to the complaint.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the appellate court's reversal of the judgment dismissing plaintiff's complaint.

Empress Casino Joliet Corp. v. Giannoulias


Docket Nos. 104586, 104587, 104590 cons.


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


231 Ill. 2d 62; 896 N.E.2d 277; 2008 Ill. LEXIS 378; 324 Ill. Dec. 491



June 5, 2008, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Empress Casino Joliet Corp. v. Giannoulias, 2008 Ill. LEXIS 892 (Ill., Sept. 22, 2008)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Empress Casino Joliet Corp. v. Giannoulias, 129 S. Ct. 2764, 174 L. Ed. 2d 270, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 4248 (U.S., 2009)
Related proceeding at Empress Casino Joliet Corp. v. Blagojevich, 674 F. Supp. 2d 993, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 113844 (N.D. Ill., 2009)
Appeal after remand at, Decision reached on appeal by Empress Casino Joliet Corp. v. Giannoulias, 2011 Ill. App. Unpub. LEXIS 53 (2011)
Related proceeding at Empress Casino Joliet Corp. v. Giannoulias, 406 Ill. App. 3d 1040, 942 N.E.2d 783, 2011 Ill. App. LEXIS 43, 347 Ill. Dec. 580 (Ill. App. Ct. 3d Dist., 2011)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the Treasurer of the State of Illinois and the Illinois Racing Board, challenged an order of the Circuit Court of Will County (Illinois), which granted plaintiff casinos summary judgment in their action seeking a declaration that 2005 Ill. Laws 94--804 (the Act), which imposed a 3 percent surcharge on riverboat casinos that had adjusted gross receipts (AGR) of over $ 200 million, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court held that the circuit court erred in holding that the Act violated the uniformity clause, Ill. Const. art. IX, § 2 (1970). The casinos failed to show that there was no real and substantial difference between all casinos and casinos with an AGR over $ 200 million. There was a reasonable relationship between the classification and object of the legislation. The legislature provided express findings regarding the necessity of the tax imposed on the casinos, and those findings had to be accorded great deference. The uniformity clause allowed subclassifications and exclusions as long as they were reasonable. Quantitative differences in AGR could be sufficient to justify a classification. The 3 percent surcharge was not subject to a takings challenge. It was not a taking of private property within the meaning of the constitutional takings clauses, U.S. Const. amend. V and Ill. Const. art. I, § 15 (1970) because it was not tied to real property. The Act did not violate the public funds clause, Ill. Const. art. VIII, § 1 (1970), because it served a public purpose. The surcharge would benefit the general well-being of society and the prosperity of the people of the State.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the judgment of the circuit court.

People v. Ross


Docket No. 103972


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


229 Ill. 2d 255; 891 N.E.2d 865; 2008 Ill. LEXIS 376; 322 Ill. Dec. 574



June 5, 2008, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Counsel Amended August 20, 2008.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Appellate Court for the First District.
People v. Ross, 223 Ill. 2d 671, 865 N.E.2d 975, 2007 Ill. LEXIS 704, 310 Ill. Dec. 255 (2007)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Illinois appellate court affirmed the trial court's ruling that allowed petitioner to file a late notice of appeal in his armed robbery case. It then found that the evidence insufficiently proved the gun involved was a dangerous weapon, and directed the trial court to convict him of simple robbery. It also directed the trial court to sentence petitioner accordingly. The state supreme court granted the State's petition for leave to appeal.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner allegedly pointed a small gun at a man and demanded the man's wallet. Petitioner was soon arrested. At the petitioner's bench trial for armed robbery, evidence indicated the gun was a pellet gun. Petitioner was convicted. Petitioner's attorney did not file a direct appeal. The trial court then denied petitioner's posttrial motion. Nearly two years later, he filed a pro se postconviction petition, alleging that his trial attorney was ineffective for not timely filing a notice of appeal and for not arguing on appeal that the pellet gun was not a dangerous weapon. The trial court found that he should be allowed to file a late notice of appeal. The appellate court affirmed and found the evidence insufficient to prove the gun was a dangerous weapon, meaning a conviction for simple robbery was proper. The state supreme court found that (1) the trial court on petitioner's postconviction relief motion was permitted to grant him leave to file a late notice of appeal to allege the constitutional claim that his attorney provided ineffective assistance of counsel for not filing a notice of appeal and (2) sufficient evidence did not support the armed robbery conviction.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court affirmed the appellate court's judgment.

Napleton v. Vill. of Hinsdale


Docket No. 105096


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


229 Ill. 2d 296; 891 N.E.2d 839; 2008 Ill. LEXIS 377; 322 Ill. Dec. 548

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Illinois appellate court entered a judgment that affirmed the trial court's dismissal of plaintiff property owner's first amended complaint that requested the trial court declare certain textual amendments made by defendant village to its zoning code facially unconstitutional as violative of substantive due process and to enjoin their enforcement. The owner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The owner had several contiguous parcels of property. The property was located in a business zoning district. People v. Perkins


Docket No. 103693


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


229 Ill. 2d 34; 890 N.E.2d 398; 2007 Ill. LEXIS 2052; 321 Ill. Dec. 676



May 27, 2008, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Modified on Denial of Rehearing May 27, 2008.


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Perkins, 2007 Ill. LEXIS 1703 (Ill., 2007)



DISPOSITION: Appellate court judgment reversed; circuit court judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State of Illinois sought review of a judgment of the appellate court (Illinois), which vacated the circuit court's dismissal of appellee prisoner's untimely postconviction petition and remanded the matter to the circuit court for further proceedings.


OVERVIEW: The circuit court dismissed the postconviction petition as untimely. The appellate court vacated the dismissal and remanded the case. The State appealed. The court held that the plain language of Ill. Sup. Ct. R. 651(c), which required amendments necessary for an adequate presentation of a petitioner's contentions, required counsel to amend the untimely pro se postconviction petition to allege any available facts showing that the delay in filing was not due to the prisoner's culpable negligence in an effort to excuse the untimely filing. The court held that the appellate court erred in finding that appointed counsel did not comply with Rule 651(c) because he filed a certificate confirming his in-person consultation with the prisoner to ascertain the prisoner's contentions of constitutional deprivations, his examination of the record, and his conclusion that no amendment to the pro se petition was necessary for an adequate presentation of the prisoner's contentions. The court's construction was consistent with its prior holdings and the purpose of Rule 651(c), which was to ensure that counsel shaped the claims into proper legal form and presents them to the court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's judgment. The court affirmed the circuit court.

Holly v. Montes


Docket No. 105415


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


231 Ill. 2d 153; 896 N.E.2d 267; 2008 Ill. LEXIS 328; 324 Ill. Dec. 481



May 22, 2008, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Holly v. Montes, 2008 Ill. LEXIS 895 (Ill., Sept. 22, 2008)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Holly v. Montes, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 1309 (U.S., Feb. 23, 2009)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Mandamus denied; complaint dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff former prisoner filed a complaint for mandamus under Ill. Sup. Ct. R. 381, seeking an order directing defendant Prisoner Review Board to eliminate the condition of electronic home confinement (EHC) during his mandatory supervised release (MSR). Prior to oral argument on his complaint, however, the prisoner's EHC was terminated, and the electronic monitoring device was removed. The Board then moved to dismiss the complaint as moot.


OVERVIEW: The prisoner claimed that the Board lacked statutory authority to impose EHC as a condition of MSR and that the Board lacked the authority to impose EHC at the time of the offenses and that any reliance on the legislature's subsequent grant of authority violated the Ex Post Facto Clause of U.S. Const. art. I, § 10. The court applied the public interest exception to the mootness doctrine and reviewed the claim, finding that the Board had discretion to impose EHC as a condition of MSR. There was no ex post facto violation because 730 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/3-3-7(a) was in effect when the prisoner committed offense and when the Board imposed EHC. The prisoner was not unconstitutionally subject to EHC while on MSR because he was still in the Department of Corrections' custody and under sentence. MSR was a mandatory part of his sentence. He was not yet entitled to full freedom. EHC during MSR did not constitute continued incarceration. He was not denied the benefit of his plea bargain because the absence of a specific admonishment that EHC could be a potential MSR condition did not change the essential terms of his plea agreement.


OUTCOME: The court denied the requested relief and dismissed the complaint.

Cinkus v. Stickney Mun. Officers Electoral Bd.


Docket No. 104471.


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


228 Ill. 2d 200; 886 N.E.2d 1011; 2008 Ill. LEXIS 1414; 319 Ill. Dec. 887



April 23, 2008, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Modified April 23, 2008.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Appellate Court, for the First District.
Cinkus v. Vill. of Stickney, 2008 Ill. LEXIS 294 (Ill., Mar. 20, 2008)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee objector filed an objection with appellee municipal electoral board to appellant candidate's name being on an election ballot. The board found that the candidate was ineligible for office pursuant to § 3.1-10-5(b) of the Illinois Municipal Code, 65 ILCS 5/3.1-10-5(b) (2006). A circuit court set aside the decision. The Appellate Court of Illinois reversed and confirmed the board's decision. The candidate appealed.


OVERVIEW: The board concluded that the candidate was not eligible to be a candidate for the elected municipal office sought under § 3.1-10-5(b) of the Illinois Municipal Code because he was indebted to the municipality in the amount of $ 100.00 by virtue of a judgment entered against him in that amount which, as of the date of the filing of a petition by an objector to the candidate's name being placed on the ballot, was unpaid. The board therefore declared that the candidate's nomination papers were invalid and ordered that his name not be printed on the ballot for the election. OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate court of appeals, which confirmed the decision of the board that the candidate was ineligible for office, was affirmed.

People v. Carpenter


Docket Nos. 103616, 103856, 103857 cons.


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


228 Ill. 2d 250; 888 N.E.2d 105; 2008 Ill. LEXIS 314; 320 Ill. Dec. 888



April 17, 2008, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Counsel Amended June 12, 2008.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Appellate Court for the First District.
People v. Carpenter, 368 Ill. App. 3d 288, 856 N.E.2d 551, 2006 Ill. App. LEXIS 877, 305 Ill. Dec. 746 (Ill. App. Ct. 1st Dist., 2006)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] No. 103616--Appellate court judgment affirmed; Nos. 103856 & 103857--Circuit court judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a circuit court found defendant guilty of violating 625 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/12-612 (2004), the Appellate Court for the First District (Illinois) declared the statute unconstitutional as violative of substantive due process guarantees. Based on that opinion, the Circuit Court of Grundy County (Illinois) dismissed indictments against two other defendants. The State appealed all three cases, which were consolidated.


OVERVIEW: Section 12-612 barred the possession or ownership of a vehicle that had a false or hidden compartment. The State claimed that nonconstitutional grounds that could have resolved the cases should have been addressed before § 12-612 was declared unconstitutional. As to the first defendant, the court found that the evidence was insufficient to sustain a conviction. The other circuit court had to follow the holding of the appellate court, whether or not it agreed with that holding. The statute required that the compartment be both intended "and designed" to be used to conceal, hide, and prevent discovery of the compartment or its contents. In each case, the compartment was designed to contain an air bag. Assuming that the purpose of the statute was the laudable goal of protecting police and punishing those who hid guns and illegal contraband from officers, the court held that the statute was not a reasonable method of accomplishing the desired objective. Because the intent of the legislature was to punish those who concealed firearms or contraband in false or secret compartments, the rational approach was to punish--via an additional felony offense--those who actually did that.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments.

Poindexter v. State


Docket No. 104853.


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


229 Ill. 2d 194; 890 N.E.2d 410; 2008 Ill. LEXIS 307; 321 Ill. Dec. 688



April 3, 2008, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Poindexter v. State, 2008 Ill. LEXIS 336 (Ill., May 27, 2008)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Poindexter v. Illinois, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 5536 (U.S., Oct. 6, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Appellate Court for the Fourth District.
Poindexter v. People, 372 Ill. App. 3d 1021, 869 N.E.2d 139, 2007 Ill. App. LEXIS 562, 311 Ill. Dec. 465 (Ill. App. Ct. 4th Dist., 2007)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant community spouses sued appellee, the State of Illinois, for declaratory and injunctive relief, asserting that the Illinois spousal support provisions were preempted by the Medicare Catastrophic Coverage Act of 1988 (MCCA), 42 U.S.C.S. § 1396r-5. The circuit court found for the spouses and enjoined the State from seeking spousal support. The Court of Appeals of Illinois reversed. The spouses filed for leave to appeal.


OVERVIEW: The State of Illinois administratively adjudged and sought spousal support from the community spouses for their institutionalized spouses. Rather than proceeding with further administrative options, the community spouses alleged that art. X of the Illinois Public Aid Code, 305 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/10-1 through 10-28 (2006), and its implementing regulations conflicted with the MCCA, and thus were preempted pursuant to the Supremacy Clause of U.S. Const. art. VI, cl. 2. The state supreme court found that the community spouses were not required to exhaust administrative remedies before they sought declaratory and injunctive relief because their action was a constitutional challenge to a statute, falling within a recognized exception to the exhaustion doctrine. Moreover, the MCCA did not preempt art. X of the Illinois Public Aid Code because, while the MCCA addressed eligibility determinations and the post-eligibility income of a potentially impoverished spouse, it said nothing about the ability of the State of Illinois to seek reimbursement from an otherwise financially secure community spouse.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court, which reversed the trial court, was affirmed.

155 of 2788 DOCUMENTS


People v. Manning


Docket No. 104300


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


227 Ill. 2d 403; 883 N.E.2d 492; 2008 Ill. LEXIS 278; 318 Ill. Dec. 261



February 7, 2008, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Appellate Court for the Second District.
People v. Manning, 371 Ill. App. 3d 457, 863 N.E.2d 289, 2007 Ill. App. LEXIS 102, 309 Ill. Dec. 59 (Ill. App. Ct. 2d Dist., 2007)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought further review after an Illinois appellate court affirmed a trial court's denial of defendant's motion to withdraw his open plea of guilty to a charge of residential burglary. Defendant argued that defense counsel was ineffective for failing to advise defendant of the possibility of pleading guilty but mentally ill (GBMI) pursuant to 725 Ill. Comp. Stat. Ann. 5/113-4(d) (2002).


OVERVIEW: As there was no plea agreement, there could be no argument that any undue influence or force was involved. Thus, there was no basis to conclude defendant's plea was involuntary. Defendant's lack of information about a GBMI plea was not sufficient to render his plea of guilty unknowing or involuntary. Further, defendant was not prejudiced by the entry of his plea, and therefore, he could not satisfy the second prong of the Strickland test for ineffective assistance of counsel. A GBMI plea would not have affected the length of defendant's sentence. Also, because the Department of Corrections was aware of defendant's mental health issues, there was virtually no difference in the treatment that defendant would receive under his guilty plea as opposed to a GBMI plea. The Eighth Amendment required that he receive adequate medical care. Likewise, under Ill. Admin. Code tit. 20, §§ 415.40, 415.30 (2007), defendant was to have access to mental health services and was to be informed of the procedures for obtaining those services. While a GBMI adjudication required the Department to examine defendant periodically, those examinations did not guarantee treatment.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court.
